Review \nAccepted: 23. 4. 2013.    UDC: 339.138:061.2&gt;(497.5) \n \n \n\nThe aim of this paper is to present the conceptual basis for examining the effects of \nactivities, performed within the \u2018traditional\u2019 nonprofit strategic marketing, on the \nfundraising success. A review of the existing theoretical and empirical studies in \nthe field of fundraising is provided. Open questions in fundraising are addressed, \nwith the special attention devoted to the identification of marketing activities that \ncould ensure higher fundraising, as well as the overall performance of nonprofit \norganizations. Based on the existing literature, it is proposed that long-term \nrelationships with donors and implementation of the \u2018traditional\u2019 marketing \nactivities are crucial to fundraising, with the fundraising serving as a feedback for \nnonprofit strategic marketing. This paper also analyzes the situation and prospects \nof fundraising in the nonprofit sector in Croatia. Further suggestions for the \nempirical verification of the proposed theoretical model are made, along with the \nrecommendations for future research. \n\n \n1. INTRODUCTION \n \nNonprofit organizations need resources to achieve organizational goals and \n\nfulfill their mission, as well as to grow and develop their activities. The \nfundamental categories of required resources are (Andreasen &amp; Kotler, 2008): \n\n\u2022 financial resources (including revenues from products and services), \n\u2022 human resources (employees and volunteers). \n\n \nAmong those, the success in raising funds is crucial to the performance of \n\nnonprofit organizations. Fundraising, as the activity directed toward securing \n\n                                                           \n*   Ljiljana Najev \u010ca\u010dija, University of Split, Faculty of Economics, Cvite Fiskovi\u0107a 5, 21000 \n\nSplit, Croatia, Phone: +385 98 299 100, E-mail: ljnajev@efst.hr    \n\n\n\nManagement, Vol. 18, 2013, 1, pp. 59-78 \nLj. Najev \u010ca\u010dija: Fundraising in the context of nonprofit strategic marketing\u2026  \n\n60 \n\nfinancial resources from donors, is difficult to define. Many authors simply state \nthat it is the collection of funds, or do not provide a definition at all, but rather \nspecify the fundraising activities and tools.  \n\n \nAndreasen and Kotler (2008) define fundraising as an activity of collecting \n\nfinancial resources and identify the main sources of funds. They emphasize that \nthe nonprofit sector (fundraising included) has gone through three orientation \nphases in its development, as related to the product, sales and marketing \norientation. Pavi\u010di\u0107 (2003) defines fundraising in terms of its activities and \nbelieves that it could be viewed not only as a part of the overall marketing \nstrategy, but also as a separate strategic and implementation strategic activity. \nSargeant et al. (2010) also do not provide a clear definition of fundraising, but \nrather emphasize the activities and processes required for its successful \nimplementation. Another significant limitation is the orientation of the existing \nliterature toward practical tools and approaches, with many authors serving as \nfundraising practitioners. Although such manuals are important for the practice, \na more strict approach, directed toward the development of fundraising \ndiscipline, is required as well. \n\n \n2. FUNDRAISING: THEORETICAL BASIS \n \nFundraising has reached a marketing orientation in its development, and it \n\ncan no longer be regarded as a request for money, based on the philanthropic \nmotives, but rather as the exchange of values, which meets the donors\u2019 needs \n(Andreasen &amp; Kotler, 2008). Unfortunately, a large number of nonprofit \norganizations do not share such a marketing orientation to fundraising, as they \nconcentrate on satisfying organizational needs. Thus, fundraising has \nincreasingly become a strategic approach to donors and establishment of long-\nterm relationships, which may not initially generate planned revenues in a short-\ntime perspective. From this viewpoint, there are two approaches to raising funds \n(Sargeant, 2001): \n\n\u2022 the transactional approach, concentrating on the immediate financial \nneeds of an organization, without \u2018anchoring\u2019 in the developing a \nstrategic plan and  \n\n\u2022 the strategic approach, based on the organization\u2019s long-term plan, \nwhich should benefit from the synergy of multiple fundraising projects \nand activities.  \n\n \nWarwick (1999) points out that fundraising can do much more than simply \n\nprovide funds for the organization, as the fundraising objectives may include \ngrowth (creating a donor base), involvement (making donors active), visibility \n\n\n\nManagement, Vol. 18, 2013, 1, pp. 59-78 \nLj. Najev \u010ca\u010dija: Fundraising in the context of nonprofit strategic marketing\u2026  \n\n61 \n\n(raising organization\u2019s public profile), efficiency (reducing the cost of \nfundraising), stability, etc. Fundamental principles of fundraising are rather \nsimple (Weinstein, 2009).  Firstly, people are the basis of the whole fundraising \nprocess, since people decide to give financial support to people (who work in \norganizations) in order to help (other people). Likewise, the importance of the \namount of a small donation should not be diminished, as people donate in \naccordance with the resources available, while the organization not valuing all \ndonated amounts, might be endangering their donor base. Furthermore, the \nleaders, staff and volunteers need to demonstrate the dedication to the \norganization by volunteer work or own donations, in order to convince the \npotential donors of the credibility to ensure the fulfillment of the objectives and \n\u201cdeserve\u201d their donations. Therefore, \u201csuccessful fundraising is the right person \nasking the right prospect for the right amount for the right project at the right \ntime in the right way.\u201d (Weinstein, op. cit., p. 4). Considering the total amount \nof collected funds and the number of donors, the Pareto principle is also \napplicable: often, 80%, or an even higher amount of funds, come from 20%, or \neven a lower number of donors. \n\n \nAlthough there is no generalized classification of fundraising sources, \n\npartly due to regional/national characteristics and regulations, sources of \nfunding can generally be (roughly) divided into four main sources (Andreasen \n&amp; Kotler, 2008): donations of individual donors, donations of profit entities, \nfor-profit and non-profit foundations; income generated by own activities and \nincome from the partnership with the private sector. Each organization has a \ndifferent ratio of funds from various sources that have been raised in various \nways. The fundraising methods can be classified in the same way as the fund \nsources, depending on the various external factors. One of the frequently quoted \nclassifications of fundraising activities is  (Sargeant et al., 2010): major gift \nfundraising; direct marketing fundraising; Internet fundraising; retention and \ndevelopment of relations with donors; bequest gift, in memoriam and tribute \ndonation; planned giving; corporate giving, and grant and foundations. \nNonprofit organizations working in different fields and with different goals \nhave different ratio of funds obtained from different sources, but most of the \norganizations depend primarily on donations.  \n\n \n3. FUNDRAISING: A REVIEW OF PRIOR RESEARCH \n \n3.1. Individual donor characteristics  \n \nMany studies tried to determine the influence of various factors on the \n\nbehavior of individual donors, both small and large. {"content_type": "text", "parts": ["### Part A: Kara Logan Berlin&#x27;s Perspective on Money and Personal Feelings\n\nKara Logan Berlin emphasizes the importance of understanding our personal relationships and feelings toward money, which is crucial for those involved in fundraising. This understanding can significantly impact our effectiveness in raising funds. Personally, my view on money is complex; I see it as a necessary tool for achieving freedom and supporting causes I believe in, yet I am also critical of the capitalist system that often prioritizes profit over community welfare. This dual perspective shapes my strategic approach to managing money, ensuring it supports meaningful initiatives without reinforcing systems I critique.\n\n### Part B: Building and Maintaining Relationships in Fundraising\n\nAccording to Hall (2002) and Najev \u010ca\u010dija (2013), successful fundraising depends on the strategic establishment and maintenance of relationships. Hall highlights the role of trust and mutual satisfaction in community college fundraising, while Najev \u010ca\u010dija combines marketing and fundraising strategies to align with donor values and needs. In my practice, I focus on authentic engagement with potential donors, listening to their values, and aligning them with the goals of my initiatives. This fosters deep, lasting partnerships rather than transactional interactions.\n\n### Part C: Strategic Elements in Fundraising\n\nFive critical elements of fundraising that require strategic planning are:\n1. **Donor Identification**: Understanding who will support the cause based on shared values.\n2. **Engagement Strategy**: Personalized communication to maintain donor interest and commitment.\n3. **Messaging and Communication**: Crafting messages that resonate with donors and articulate the impact of their contributions.\n4. **Event Planning**: Ensuring events provide value to attendees and effectively showcase the cause.\n5. **Metrics for Success**: Establishing metrics to track progress and adjust strategies as needed.\n\nEach of these elements must be meticulously planned and executed to build and sustain donor relationships, ultimately supporting the broader goals of the initiative (Weinstein, 2002).\n\n### Part A: Essential Elements of Sustainable Fundraising\n\nTwo essential elements of sustainable fundraising are:\n1. **Longevity of Relationships**: As Nigel Harris from the Mater Foundation points out, nurturing long-term relationships enhances donor retention and organizational stability (Efrat &amp; Harris, 2017).\n2. **Compelling Case Statement**: A clear case statement aligns fundraising efforts with organizational goals, helping donors understand the significance of their contributions (Hartford Foundation, 2011).\n\nThese elements help build a sustainable fundraising approach where donors feel connected to the cause, fostering enduring support.\n\n### Part B: Differences Between Fundraising and Sustainable Fundraising\n\nThe main differences are:\n1. **Strategic Focus**: Traditional fundraising targets immediate financial gains, often leading to one-off campaigns. Sustainable fundraising focuses on long-term donor relationships and retention, which is more cost-effective and valuable.\n2. **Depth of Relationships**: Sustainable fundraising prioritizes ongoing engagement and communication, creating a loyal donor base and deeper commitment to the organization\u2019s mission (Weinstein, 2002; Waters, 2009).\n\n### Part C: Insights from the 2020 Nonprofit Leadership Impact Study\n\n**Surprising**: The significant emphasis on technology and innovation in strategic planning, reflecting a shift towards digital transformation in the nonprofit sector (Te, 2020).\n\n**Relevant**: The focus on leadership development and succession planning is crucial for establishing strong frameworks early in organizational development (Te, 2020).\n\n**Helpful**: Insights on effective communication and donor transparency are vital for building trust and long-term relationships with donors (Te, 2020).\n\n### Key Takeaways from &quot;Fundraising in the Context of Nonprofit Strategic Marketing&quot;\n\nLjiljana Najev \u010ca\u010dija highlights the crucial role of long-term donor relationships and traditional marketing activities in successful fundraising. This strategic approach transforms fundraising from merely seeking donations to an exchange of values that meets donors&#x27; needs. The paper emphasizes the need for nonprofit organizations to adopt a marketing-oriented approach to fundraising, ensuring sustained support and long-term growth (Najev \u010ca\u010dija, 2013)."]}, "tether_quote", "url": "file-pLh6MLQ1qRV9B8yMjrwyk6Ai", "domain": "How Grantmakers Can Use Power Mindfully to Advance Equity.pdf", "text": "\nPower in Philanthropy\n\nFoundations\n\nHow Grantmakers Can Use Power Mindfully to\nAdvance Equity\nWhile there are many potential barriers to utilizing power ethically and responsibly, funders can\u2014and must\u2014\novercome them to truly advance equity and justice.\n\nBy Lisa Ranghelli Oct. 31, 2018\n\nLike air, power is everywhere, yet it is often invisible. We may not think about its role in our daily lives,\n\nbut we live and breathe it. Used positively, power helps communities thrive. But there\u2019s also a dark side.\n\nLike polluted air, power becomes most visible when something is wrong\u2014when someone abuses it, and\n\nthe consequences are inescapable. Foundations often wait to acknowledge inequitable power dynamics\n\nand their impact until events force them to\u2014a police shooting of an unarmed black person in their\n\ncommunity, or a parent deported and their family torn apart. Once power is visible, we see how race,\n\ngender, wealth, and other identi\ufffders in\ufffduence who has power over whom.\n\nhttps://ssir.org/power_in_philanthropy\nhttps://ssir.org/articles/category/foundations\nhttps://ssir.org/\n\n\nThis series, presented in partnership with the National Committee for Responsive\nPhilanthropy, aims to explore popular concepts in philanthropy\u2014such as risk, capacity\nbuilding, and public leadership\u2014through the lens of power and equitable outcomes.\n\n FOLLOW THIS SERIES\n\nBut even unseen power can do harm, sometimes unwittingly, as when our health system treats white\n\npeople better than people of color or our school funding formula disadvantages low-income students.\n\nIndeed, it lurks behind every major disparity in access or outcomes for marginalized people.\n\nIt also hides in foundations\u2019 bank accounts and boardrooms, in every meeting with a grant applicant or\n\ngrant partner, in every community meeting or city council meeting. It can be found in grantmaking\n\npractices that inadvertently favor some applicants over others.\n\nThree reasons why funders don\u2019t face power head on\n\nThe best way to deal with power constructively and e\ufffdectively is to acknowledge it even when it seems\n\ninvisible. Yet many funders don\u2019t. What keeps them from dealing directly with power as a driving force\n\nin our communities?\n\nSince the National Committee for Responsive Philanthropy released our toolkit for building, sharing,\n\nand wielding power, I have observed three barriers commonly expressed by funders themselves:\n\n1. Foundations lack enough institutional buy-in.\n\nPeople tell us their leaders haven\u2019t all bought into the idea that power is essential to advance equity. Can\n\nnon-executive sta\ufffd make progress without the CEO\u2019s support? Or can the CEO tackle power issues\n\nwithout the board\u2019s approval?\n\nAs Michelle McMurray, senior program o\ufffdcer of health and human services at the Pittsburgh\n\nFoundation candidly shared in a recent webinar on sharing power:\n\nSometimes the seat we sit in, we\u2019re kind of in between; we may feel like we don\u2019t have the power\n\nto make the change. Someone\u2019s going to tell us \u201cNot now. Not ever.\u201d But do the \ufffdrst, next thing\n\nyou can do\u2014you do have some power. Find that, and channel it, and use it for all it\u2019s worth.\n\nBecause the only other option is to do nothing.\n\nhttps://www.ncrp.org/?utm_source=SSIR\nhttps://www.ncrp.org/?utm_source=SSIR\nhttps://www.ncrp.org/?utm_source=SSIR\nhttps://www.ncrp.org/initiatives/philamplify/power-moves-philanthropy?utm_source=SSIR\nhttps://www.ncrp.org/initiatives/philamplify/power-moves-philanthropy?utm_source=SSIR\nhttps://www.ncrp.org/2018/09/philanthropy-experts-swap-tips-on-sharing-power.html?utm_source=SSIR\n\n\nMcMurray was speaking to fellow program o\ufffdcers, but she could just as easily have been addressing a\n\nCEO, a vice president, a grants manager, an evaluator, or a communications chief. Everyone within an\n\ninstitution has some point of leverage, some opportunity to act.\n\nDuring a di\ufffderent webinar introducing our toolkit, Marcelo Bonta, principal at the Raben group,\n\nencouraged a small group of change agents to re\ufffdect and experiment in one program area or part of the\n\nfoundation, then to begin to make changes within their sphere and gradually in\ufffduence other parts of\n\nthe institution.\n\nFor example, a grants manager can develop more inclusive application processes by asking nonpro\ufffdt\n\nleaders of color what prevents them from applying for a grant. A program o\ufffdcer can begin building\n\ncommunity power by funding the capacity of grant partners to participate in coalitions. A trustee can\n\nadvocate for using the foundation\u2019s bully pulpit more often.\n\n2. Funders aren\u2019t far enough along on diversity, equity, and inclusion (DEI).\n\nMany foundations are beginning to look at the extent to which their sta\ufffd, board, and internal practices\n\nre\ufffdect DEI values, which requires an examination of dominant white culture. Funders may be hesitant\n\nto focus on how to use power externally to advance equity while this internal process is still underway or\n\nhas not yet begun. One attendee of our toolkit introduction webinar noted:\n\nWe\u2019ve made progress\u2014we&#x27;ve developed communications resources so that we speak in a\n\nconsistent way about equity in our work. Yet there\u2019s no e\ufffdort to operationalize equity through\n\nchanging processes and practices. Equity isn\u2019t \u201cin the water\u201d yet. For example, we don\u2019t have\n\nhonest conversations about the white, male, cis-, straight, Western norms in our organization.\n\nCan you build, share, and wield power externally if your internal house is not in order?\n\nAnswers to this question depend on each institution\u2019s situation, and where board and sta\ufffd express the\n\nmost energy or urgency to strengthen DEI practices. Some funders do internal work \ufffdrst, some start\n\nexternally, and some work on both simultaneously.\n\nDuring the same webinar, Gita Gulati-Partee, who consults extensively on equity issues as founder and\n\nprincipal of OpenSource Leadership Strategies, noted that a funder can \u201cturn o\ufffd strategy but not\n\nhttps://www.ncrp.org/2018/06/why-examining-power-and-privilege-is-critical-to-shifting-culture-in-philanthropy.html?utm_source=SSIR\nhttp://www.opensourceleadership.com/about.htm\n\n\nculture.\u201d Yet the two are inseparable\u2014the hip bone is connected to the thigh bone, so to speak. Thus, it\n\nmay be easier to take a break from strategy to focus on internal culture, but eventually you\u2019ll \ufffdnd your\n\nway back to strategy.\n\nRegardless of which path you take, it\u2019s important to think through and communicate the timing and\n\nsequencing of the foundation\u2019s process for addressing internal and external aspects of power and equity\n\nwith sta\ufffd, board, and constituents in ways that are intentional and transparent. And know that each path\n\nwill take time and deep e\ufffdort; board and sta\ufffd will need to put o\ufffd other responsibilities to explore power\n\nmeaningfully.\n\n3. Foundations are afraid to take greater risks.\n\nAs philanthropic advisor Allen Smart recently noted, philanthropy is generally risk-averse, even though\n\nit has more freedom to act boldly and innovate than other sectors of society. CEOs may fear what will\n\nhappen if they challenge their boards to have tough conversations about power, or if they ask their grant\n\npartners for honest feedback. Trustees often see their role as \ufffdnancial stewards and don\u2019t want to\n\njeopardize their foundation\u2019s endowment. Financial and legal advisors tend toward caution, reinforcing\n\nrisk aversion. Leaders may fear harm to their own reputation and their institution\u2019s if they take a risk\n\nand fail.\n\nThese are all real and legitimate concerns. However, funders must weigh them against the risks\n\nmarginalized communities face every day just trying to get by, as well as when they try to speak truth to\n\npower, whether that\u2019s policymakers or foundations. Funders must also consider the risk of inaction\u2014of\n\nletting our communities continue to su\ufffder, or of failing to achieve equity goals and perpetuating the\n\nstatus quo.\n\nWe all engage in power dynamics all the time, whether or not we are aware or acknowledge it. We may\n\nunconsciously enable power for some and disable power for others, causing harm or missing\n\nopportunities to use power for good. It\u2019s time for us to meditate on power so that we can be more\n\nconscious about how each of us manifests it\u2014to notice who bene\ufffdts and toward what end. In doing so,\n\nthe obstacles to using it e\ufffdectively will become surmountable and inspire mindful action.\n\nSupport\u00a0SSIR\u2019s coverage of cross-sector solutions to global challenges.\u00a0\n\nhttps://www.ncrp.org/2018/08/philanthropic-risk-its-personal.html?utm_source=SSIR\n\n\nHelp us further the reach of innovative ideas.\u00a0Donate today.\n\nRead more stories by toolkit \u201cPower Moves: Your essential\n\nphilanthropy assessment guide for equity and justice.\u201d\n\nDOI: 10.48558/cypv-gt68\n\nIf you like this article enough to print it, be sure to subscribe By 1968, spurred on by such critiques\n\nand the urgency of advancing racial equity, the foundation had helped pioneer an innovative tool\n\nintended to unlock new philanthropic resources: program-related investments (PRIs). Early on, the\n\nfoundation committed $10 million to PRIs, and among the \ufffdrst initiatives was Rev. Leon Sullivan\u2019s\n\nProgress Plaza, the \ufffdrst shopping center in the United States owned and run by African Americans. By\n\ndirecting capital to communities and entrepreneurs long denied it, PRIs could complement the\n\nfoundation\u2019s broader strategy for economic empowerment, racial equity, and social justice.\n\nIn the 50 years since the development of PRIs, the Ford Foundation\u2019s \ufffdght for racial equity and our use\n\nof new impact investing tools have gone hand in hand. As time has passed and tools and circumstances\n\nhave changed, we\u2019ve continuously looked for new ways to answer a version of that original question:\n\nHow do we get more capital to the people and communities who truly need it?\n\nBeginning in the 1980s\u2014in an e\ufffdort to confront inequalities caused by racially biased bank practices,\n\ndiscrimination, and redlining\u2014we made grants and PRIs to develop and scale the community\n\ndevelopment \ufffdnancial institution (CDFI) and a\ufffdordable housing industries. Today, we use many types\n\nhttps://ssir.org/how_foundations_are_using_impact_investing_to_advance_racial_equity\n\n\nof resources to address inequality, and speci\ufffdcally racial and gender inequality, in all its forms. These\n\ninclude grants in our gender, racial, and ethnic justice program; PRIs; and mission-related investments\n\ncommitted from our endowment. While our philanthropic investment used to focus on only 5 percent of\n\nour resources, we are beginning to unlock the other 95 percent toward this goal, with a recent\n\ncommitment to allocate up to $1 billion of the endowment for a new mission-related investment\n\nstrategy.\n\nTwo Interrelated Gaps: Investment and Investment\nManagers\n\nWithout adequate capital, entrepreneurs cannot develop their ideas and products into sustainable, well-\n\norganized enterprises that bring meaningful value to them and their customers. Yet\u2014as others have\n\nthoughtfully considered throughout this series\u2014when it comes to receiving venture capital and private\n\nequity, founders of color are not given the same support as their white counterparts. By 1968, spurred on by such critiques\n\nand the urgency of advancing racial equity, the foundation had helped pioneer an innovative tool\n\nintended to unlock new philanthropic resources: program-related investments (PRIs). Early on, the\n\nfoundation committed $10 million to PRIs, and among the \ufffdrst initiatives was Rev. Leon Sullivan\u2019s\n\nProgress Plaza, the \ufffdrst shopping center in the United States owned and run by African Americans. By\n\ndirecting capital to communities and entrepreneurs long denied it, PRIs could complement the\n\nfoundation\u2019s broader strategy for economic empowerment, racial equity, and social justice.\n\nIn the 50 years since the development of PRIs, the Ford Foundation\u2019s \ufffdght for racial equity and our use\n\nof new impact investing tools have gone hand in hand. As time has passed and tools and circumstances\n\nhave changed, we\u2019ve continuously looked for new ways to answer a version of that original question:\n\nHow do we get more capital to the people and communities who truly need it?\n\nBeginning in the 1980s\u2014in an e\ufffdort to confront inequalities caused by racially biased bank practices,\n\ndiscrimination, and redlining\u2014we made grants and PRIs to develop and scale the community\n\ndevelopment \ufffdnancial institution (CDFI) and a\ufffdordable housing industries. Today, we use many types\n\nhttps://ssir.org/how_foundations_are_using_impact_investing_to_advance_racial_equity\n\n\nof resources to address inequality, and speci\ufffdcally racial and gender inequality, in all its forms. These\n\ninclude grants in our gender, racial, and ethnic justice program; PRIs; and mission-related investments\n\ncommitted from our endowment. While our philanthropic investment used to focus on only 5 percent of\n\nour resources, we are beginning to unlock the other 95 percent toward this goal, with a recent\n\ncommitment to allocate up to $1 billion of the endowment for a new mission-related investment\n\nstrategy.\n\nTwo Interrelated Gaps: Investment and Investment\nManagers\n\nWithout adequate capital, entrepreneurs cannot develop their ideas and products into sustainable, well-\n\norganized enterprises that bring meaningful value to them and their customers. Yet\u2014as others have\n\nthoughtfully considered throughout this series\u2014when it comes to receiving venture capital and private\n\nequity, founders of color are not given the same support as their white counterparts. We hope other foundations, pension funds, sovereign\n\nwealth funds, and other institutional investors will join us in this pursuit of justice and greater returns,\n\nand step forward to ask the hard questions\u2014as Robert Ross and Amy Chung of The California\n\nEndowment note in this series\u2014to achieve meaningful organizational change. We are heartened by the\n\nrecent announcement by the Kresge Foundation to move 25 percent of its assets under the control of\n\nwomen and \ufffdrms led by people of color by 2025. Together, we can address these gaps at every level, and\n\ndirect our resources toward greater equity, prosperity, and progress.\n\nSupport\u00a0SSIR\u2019s coverage of cross-sector solutions to global challenges.\u00a0\n\nHelp us further the reach of innovative ideas.\u00a0Donate today.\n\nRead more stories by Roy Swan &amp; Darren Walker.\n\nRoy Swan is director of mission investments at the Ford Foundation. By 1968, spurred on by such critiques\n\nand the urgency of advancing racial equity, the foundation had helped pioneer an innovative tool\n\nintended to unlock new philanthropic resources: program-related investments (PRIs). Early on, the\n\nfoundation committed $10 million to PRIs, and among the \ufffdrst initiatives was Rev. Leon Sullivan\u2019s\n\nProgress Plaza, the \ufffdrst shopping center in the United States owned and run by African Americans. By\n\ndirecting capital to communities and entrepreneurs long denied it, PRIs could complement the\n\nfoundation\u2019s broader strategy for economic empowerment, racial equity, and social justice.\n\nIn the 50 years since the development of PRIs, the Ford Foundation\u2019s \ufffdght for racial equity and our use\n\nof new impact investing tools have gone hand in hand. As time has passed and tools and circumstances\n\nhave changed, we\u2019ve continuously looked for new ways to answer a version of that original question:\n\nHow do we get more capital to the people and communities who truly need it?\n\nBeginning in the 1980s\u2014in an e\ufffdort to confront inequalities caused by racially biased bank practices,\n\ndiscrimination, and redlining\u2014we made grants and PRIs to develop and scale the community\n\ndevelopment \ufffdnancial institution (CDFI) and a\ufffdordable housing industries. Today, we use many types\n\nhttps://ssir.org/how_foundations_are_using_impact_investing_to_advance_racial_equity\n\n\nof resources to address inequality, and speci\ufffdcally racial and gender inequality, in all its forms. These\n\ninclude grants in our gender, racial, and ethnic justice program; PRIs; and mission-related investments\n\ncommitted from our endowment. While our philanthropic investment used to focus on only 5 percent of\n\nour resources, we are beginning to unlock the other 95 percent toward this goal, with a recent\n\ncommitment to allocate up to $1 billion of the endowment for a new mission-related investment\n\nstrategy.\n\nTwo Interrelated Gaps: Investment and Investment\nManagers\n\nWithout adequate capital, entrepreneurs cannot develop their ideas and products into sustainable, well-\n\norganized enterprises that bring meaningful value to them and their customers. Yet\u2014as others have\n\nthoughtfully considered throughout this series\u2014when it comes to receiving venture capital and private\n\nequity, founders of color are not given the same support as their white counterparts. This approach recognizes that capital can be a powerful tool for addressing some of the world&#x27;s most pressing challenges.\n\n#### Key Points from the Attachments\n\n1. **Historical Context and Evolution (Ford Foundation)**\n   - The Ford Foundation has a long history of impact investing, starting from the Civil Rights Movement in the 1960s.\n   - Initially, the foundation supported individual leaders and institutions through grants but later recognized the need for more direct investment in marginalized communities.\n   - The Ford Foundation\u2019s impact investing efforts include the development of shopping centers owned and operated by African Americans, such as Progress Plaza in Philadelphia\u30107\u2020source\u3011.\n\n2. **Current Initiatives and Strategies**\n   - The foundation focuses on integrating racial equity into its impact investing strategies.\n   - Emphasis on hiring diverse managers and investing in communities of color.\n   - The use of program-related investments (PRIs) and community development financial institutions (CDFIs) to drive investments that support racial and gender equity.\n   - Encouragement of partnerships that prioritize the inclusion of marginalized groups in economic opportunities\u30107\u2020source\u3011.\n\n3. **Long-term Goals**\n   - The Ford Foundation aims to unlock more capital and achieve greater justice and equity through its investments.\n   - The strategy involves not just immediate financial returns but also long-term societal benefits, particularly in advancing racial equity and justice\u30107\u2020source\u3011.\n\n### General Concepts and Principles of Impact Investing\n\n1. **Dual Objectives**\n   - Impact investors seek to achieve both financial returns and positive social or environmental outcomes.\n   - This dual objective distinguishes impact investing from traditional investing, which primarily focuses on financial returns.\n\n2. **Measurement and Reporting**\n   - Impact investing requires rigorous measurement and reporting of both financial performance and social/environmental impact.\n   - Tools and frameworks such as the Global Impact Investing Rating System (GIIRS) and the Impact Reporting and Investment Standards (IRIS) are commonly used.\n\n3. **Sectors and Themes**\n   - Impact investments span various sectors, including renewable energy, education, healthcare, affordable housing, and microfinance.\n   - Common themes include sustainability, poverty alleviation, gender equality, and racial equity.\n\n4. **Types of Impact Investments**\n   - **Equity Investments:** Providing capital to enterprises in exchange for ownership stakes.\n   - **Debt Investments:** Offering loans or credit to organizations with the expectation of repayment with interest.\n   - **Program-Related Investments (PRIs):** Investments that align with philanthropic missions and often come with concessionary terms.\n   - **Social Impact Bonds (SIBs):** Pay-for-success contracts where investors are repaid based on the achievement of specific social outcomes.\n\n5. **Stakeholders in Impact Investing**\n   - **Foundations and Philanthropic Organizations:** Use endowment funds to invest in social causes.\n   - **Institutional Investors:** Pension funds, insurance companies, and other large investors increasingly incorporate impact investments into their portfolios.\n   - **Individual Investors:** High-net-worth individuals and retail investors are also participating through impact investment funds and platforms.\n   - **Government and Multilateral Organizations:** Support impact investing through policy, funding, and partnerships.\n\n6. **Challenges and Considerations**\n   - **Measuring Impact:** Quantifying social and environmental outcomes can be complex and context-specific.\n   - **Balancing Returns:** Ensuring that financial returns do not compromise the social/environmental mission.\n   - **Market Development:** Building the necessary infrastructure and markets to support impact investing.\n   - **Risk Management:** Navigating risks unique to impact investing, such as political or regulatory changes.\n\n### Examples of Impact Investing in Practice\n\n- **Renewable Energy Projects:** Investing in solar, wind, and other renewable energy projects to combat climate change while generating returns.\n- **Affordable Housing:** Financing the development of affordable housing units to address homelessness and housing insecurity.\n- **Microfinance:** Providing small loans to entrepreneurs in developing countries to spur economic development and reduce poverty.\n- **Healthcare Initiatives:** Funding healthcare startups or community health programs to improve access to medical services in underserved areas.\n\n### Conclusion\n\nImpact investing represents a powerful approach to using capital for social good, combining the rigor of financial investment with the mission-driven goals of philanthropy. As demonstrated by the Ford Foundation\u2019s initiatives, it requires a deep commitment to equity, strategic partnerships, and a focus on long-term societal benefits. This investment strategy is increasingly recognized as essential for addressing global challenges and fostering inclusive economic growth."]}, "status": "finished_successfully", "end_turn": true, "weight": 1.0, "metadata": {"citations": [{"start_ix": 890, "end_ix": 900, "citation_format_type": "tether_og", "metadata": {"type": "file", "name": "Capital for Justice_ Spurring On Impact Investing for Racial Equity.pdf", "id": "file-0RaHilg0q3LxOiw0SyQ6OMGV", "source": "my_files", "text": "\nRev. The dual objectives of impact investing mean that investors are seeking a blend of these returns, which can vary in emphasis depending on the specific goals of the investment. Here&#x27;s a deeper look at both types of returns in impact investing:\n\n### Financial Returns\n\n- **Market-Rate Returns**: Some impact investments aim to achieve market-rate financial returns comparable to traditional investments. These are suitable for investors who want to align their portfolios with their values without sacrificing financial performance.\n- **Below-Market Returns**: Other impact investments might accept below-market returns in exchange for greater social or environmental impact. This approach is common in areas where the primary objective is social good, and the financial return is secondary.\n\n### Social and Environmental Returns\n\n- **Quantitative Metrics**: These are specific, measurable outcomes that the investment aims to achieve. Examples include:\n  - **Reduction in Homelessness**: An investment might aim to reduce homelessness in a city by providing affordable housing solutions.\n  - **Access to Clean Water**: Ensuring that a certain number of communities gain access to clean drinking water.\n  - **Job Creation**: Creating a specific number of jobs in underserved areas.\n  - **Educational Attainment**: Increasing graduation rates in schools serving low-income populations.\n\n- **Qualitative Metrics**: These outcomes might be more subjective but are equally important. Examples include:\n  - **Community Empowerment**: Empowering local communities through participatory development projects.\n  - **Health Improvements**: Enhancing the overall health and well-being of a community through healthcare initiatives.\n  - **Environmental Conservation**: Protecting and preserving natural habitats and biodiversity.\n\n### Examples of Impact Investing with Social Returns\n\n1. **Affordable Housing Projects**:\n   - **Financial Return**: Rental income and potential appreciation of property value.\n   - **Social Return**: Reduction in homelessness and improved living conditions for low-income families.\n\n2. **Renewable Energy Investments**:\n   - **Financial Return**: Profits from the sale of electricity generated by solar or wind farms.\n   - **Environmental Return**: Reduction in greenhouse gas emissions and dependence on fossil fuels.\n\n3. **Microfinance Institutions**:\n   - **Financial Return**: Interest and repayment on microloans.\n   - **Social Return**: Economic empowerment of small entrepreneurs and reduction of poverty in developing regions.\n\n4. **Education Technology Ventures**:\n   - **Financial Return**: Revenue from sales of educational software and services.\n   - **Social Return**: Enhanced educational outcomes and greater access to quality education for underserved students.\n\n### Measuring Social and Environmental Impact\n\nTo ensure that impact investments achieve their intended social or environmental returns, investors often use frameworks and tools such as:\n\n- **Impact Reporting and Investment Standards (IRIS)**: A system for measuring, managing, and optimizing impact.\n- **Global Impact Investing Rating System (GIIRS)**: Provides comparable ratings for impact investing funds and companies.\n- **Social Return on Investment (SROI)**: A method for measuring extra-financial value (such as social and environmental value) relative to resources invested.\n\n### Conclusion\n\nIn impact investing, returns are not solely fiduciary; they encompass both financial performance and measurable social or environmental outcomes. The balance between these types of returns depends on the goals of the investor and the specific nature of the investment. This dual focus makes impact investing a unique and powerful approach for addressing global challenges while also generating financial returns."]}, "status": "finished_successfully", "end_turn": true, "file-EwZ0yjzuRAIqqUMRDwsllzaG", "domain": "SRM DQ4.docx", "text": "Part 1: What are the three dimensions of improvement in Life Cycle Sustainability Assessment suggested in the reading? Describe why these are important and relate and apply the three dimensions to your learning from earlier modules.According to Onat et al. (2017) three key dimensions of improvement are suggested to enhance the effectiveness of sustainability assessments. These dimensions include the integration of environmental economic and social dimensions the application of systems thinking and the active engagement of stakeholders. Each of these elements is vital for constructing a holistic and effective approach to sustainability that transcends the limitations often imposed by capitalist systems which typically prioritize profit at the expense of environmental and social equity.\nThe overall approach to sustainability presented by Onat et al. (2017) emphasizes the necessity of considering the environmental economic and social impacts of products or services throughout their lifecycle. It stands in contrast to and challenges the capitalist tendency to focus solely on economic gains by highlighting the interconnectedness of these dimensions. Ignoring any of these aspects can lead to solutions that might appear beneficial in one area but are detrimental in others.Systems thinking requires understanding the complete lifecycle of a product or service\u2014from raw material extraction to disposal\u2014and identifying potential impacts at each stage. This method helps in pinpointing critical points for intervention and prevents unintended consequences that could arise from changes made in isolation. One can clearly see the links between systems thinking and the ideas presented by Ostrum (1997)  which expands the rational choice models to incorporate collective actions that better address complex social dilemmas. It challenges simplistic linear thinking\u2014common in capitalist approaches\u2014that often overlook long-term consequences for immediate profit.\nFinally engaging stakeholders across the lifecycle of a product or service ensures that the diverse values and needs of different groups are considered and addressed in the sustainability assessment process. This approach is crucial for democratizing decision-making processes which often are controlled top-down in capitalist structures. Fisher\u2019s (2020) discussions on how system dynamics modeling can involve stakeholders in understanding and managing complex systems effectively provide a solid argument for stakeholder engagement across the lifecycle. \nBy applying these three dimensions to the broader discussions from earlier modules it becomes evident how these principles can help counteract the equity and sustainability challenges exacerbated by capitalist systems. These dimensions advocate for a more inclusive holistic and long-term approach to sustainability that aligns with social equity and environmental stewardship rather than focusing narrowly on immediate economic outcomes. This integrated approach not only critiques but actively challenges the inequities perpetuated by capitalist systems paving the way for more sustainable and equitable global practices.\n\nPart 2: What indicators are required to assess the systems dynamics of flow-limited resources? \nTo effectively assess the dynamics of flow-limited resources within a system a systems dynamics model must incorporate several key indicators. These indicators include stocks flows converters/constants and information flows each playing a crucial role in understanding and predicting system behaviors.\nStocks represent the quantities of resources available within the system at any given time. These could be tangible elements like water in a reservoir or abstract quantities such as carbon or even a population count in an ecological study. Stocks are foundational for monitoring resource levels and serve as a baseline for measuring changes over time (Reuter 2013)\nFlows describe the movement of resources between stocks or from external sources into the system. This includes inputs and outputs measured over specific time intervals such as liters of water per hour or tons of carbon per year. Understanding flows is critical for assessing how resources are utilized and replenished within the system providing insights into sustainability and efficiency (Reuter 2013).\nConverters/constants are parameters within the model that affect flows or stocks but remain unchanged regardless of system dynamics. These could include growth rate constants conversion efficiencies or fixed loss rates which are essential for stabilizing the model and making accurate predictions (Reuter 2013).\nInformation flows represent the non-material connections that influence system components by altering flows or converters based on the state of stocks or other variables. This aspect of systems dynamics is crucial for modeling feedback mechanisms and adaptive behaviors within the system allowing for a more nuanced understanding of how changes in one part of the system can ripple through to others (Reuter 2013).\nBy integrating these components into a systems dynamics model it is possible to construct a comprehensive view of how resources are interlinked and governed by various dynamic factors. This holistic approach is vital for predicting future system states under different scenarios and for making informed decisions about resource management and conservation.\nWhat inputs might be incorporated into a model of flow-limited resources? How is this different from stock-limited resources? In resource management two distinct modeling approaches are used to predict and understand resource availability and sustainability: flow-limited and stock-limited models.\nFlow-limited models focus on the dynamics of resource renewal and consumption. These models consider key inputs like the rate at which a resource regenerates whether naturally or through human efforts. They also analyze the consumption rate to see how quickly the resource is being used by various entities or processes. Additionally factors like environmental conditions regulatory policies and technological changes play significant roles in influencing resource availability and flow. This model is dynamic adapting to changes in usage patterns and replenishment strategies providing a flexible framework for forecasting future resource statuses (Reuter 2013).\nIn contrast stock-limited models are concerned with the finite quantities of resources available. They focus on the absolute amount of the resource that exists or can be accessed such as fossil fuels or minerals. These models consider extraction or harvest rates detailing how quickly the resource is depleted over time. They also evaluate the resource\u2019s regeneration capabilities whether it can be naturally replenished or artificially sustained over long periods. This approach emphasizes the depletion of a fixed stock highlighting the limits of availability and the critical need for conservation and efficient use (Reuter 2013).\nThe main difference between these two models lies in their focus and application. Flow-limited models are dynamic reflecting continuous changes in resource flows and the effects of various influencing factors. They are particularly useful when resources can be renewed or are affected by external conditions. On the other hand stock-limited models deal with resources that have fixed availability concentrating on managing finite supplies and preventing exhaustion. Understanding these differences is crucial for policymakers businesses and conservationists. It helps in developing effective strategies for resource management ensuring sustainability and tackling the challenges posed by limited natural resources (Fisher 2020).\nProvide an example of the systems dynamics of:\nA stock-limited resource (such as cobalt)\nA flow-limited resource (such as rabbits)\nDescribe what and how you would measure the inputs outputs and feedback loops (in-flow out-flow connectors or dependencies stocks and sources or sinks) in each example. Keep it simple (i.e. bamboo production for chairs). You don\u2019t have to do the mathematical calculations just describe the systems process. These companies often source water from natural springs which can profoundly impact local water tables and ecosystems. The industry\u2019s reach is extensive with products distributed worldwide contributing to local economies through job creation and tax revenues. However the large-scale extraction of water raises concerns about the long-term sustainability of water resources necessitating a shift towards more sustainable practices (Spiliotopoulou &amp; Roseland 2020).\nImpact Assessment\nEnvironmental Impacts\nBottled water companies like Sanpellegrino&#x27;s operational practices particularly those surrounding large-scale water extraction from natural springs are critical to its production process. This substantial extraction is necessitated by the high demand for bottled water a trend that shows no signs of abating (Spiliotopoulou &amp; Roseland 2020). However this practice raises significant concerns about the long-term sustainability of local water resources. Extracting such large volumes of water can deplete natural springs disrupt local ecosystems and exacerbate water scarcity issues particularly in regions already facing water stress (Spiliotopoulou &amp; Roseland 2020).\nIn light of these concerns it is imperative for companies like Sanpellegrino to explore alternative water sources and implement more sustainable water management practices. This could include investing in water conservation technologies improving the efficiency of water extraction processes and engaging in corporate responsibility initiatives that prioritize environmental sustainability (Spiliotopoulou &amp; Roseland 2020). By doing so Sanpellegrino can ensure its operations&#x27; sustainability while contributing to broader environmental conservation efforts.\nEnvironment Impacts\nEnvironmental impacts of the bottled water industry include the depletion of aquifers reduced stream flows and degradation of aquatic ecosystems. Intensive water extraction can lead to declines in local water tables affecting both human use and ecosystem health (Cuoco et al. 2015).  Furthermore the extraction process can disrupt the natural balance of aquatic ecosystems leading to biodiversity loss and habitat degradation (van Engelenburg et al. 2018)\nIn addition the extraction process affects the quantity and quality of water available in local streams and rivers. Reduced water flow can lead to higher concentrations of pollutants and increased water temperatures adversely affecting aquatic life. Lower water tables can result in reduced soil moisture impacting local agriculture and vegetation (Bartolini et al. 2007; Ferragina 2010).\nThe ecological consequences of water extraction are further compounded by the reduced ability of the environment to recharge depleted aquifers. Natural recharge processes are often slower than the extraction rate leading to long-term sustainability issues. Over time this imbalance can result in permanent changes to the local ecosystem including the loss of biodiversity and the degradation of natural habitats (van Engelenburg et al. 2018).\nSocial Impacts\nThe social impacts of water bottling operations are significant affecting local communities in various ways. One of the most pressing social issues is the reduced access to water for local communities. As companies like Sanpellegrino extract vast quantities of water local residents often find their wells drying up or experiencing reduced water pressure. This can lead to conflicts over water use particularly in regions where water is already scarce (Ostrom 1998). While the water bottling industry generates substantial revenue the economic benefits often do not extend to local populations. Residents may face increased costs for water and might need to purchase bottled water for their own use effectively buying back their own resources at a premium price (Manzellaa et al. 2018).\nReduced water availability has significant health implications. Lower water quality can lead to a higher incidence of waterborne diseases impacting public health. Additionally reduced water availability affects agriculture a primary livelihood for many residents. Farmers may experience lower crop yields and increased difficulty maintaining livestock further exacerbating economic inequalities (D&#x27;ippoliti et al. 2015).\nEconomic Impacts\nThe economic impacts of the water bottling industry present a complex picture of benefits and costs (Bartolini et al. 2007). While the industry contributes to national economies by generating substantial revenue through taxes and employment it also incurs external costs related to environmental degradation and social issues (Bartolini et al. 2007; Sanpellegrino 2023).\nFor instance Sanpellegrino a major player in the industry reported revenues of over one billion Euros in 2022 significantly contributing to local and national economies (Sanpellegrino 2023). Moreover the bottling industry provides stable employment opportunities which can significantly benefit regions with limited job prospects. Sanpellegrino alone employs thousands of workers in its facilities and Bartolini et al. (2007) underscored the far-reaching effects of such employment opportunities. The study highlighted the positive implications of stable job opportunities provided by companies like Sanpellegrino supporting individuals and families with reliable incomes and stimulating local economic activity through increased consumer spending investment and tax revenues (Bartolini et al. 2007).\nHowever external costs such as environmental degradation and social costs are not typically accounted for in the industry&#x27;s economic benefits. Local governments may incur additional expenses in managing water resources addressing health impacts and mitigating environmental damage caused by over-extraction (Cuoco et al. 2015). Such realities highlight the need to comprehensively assess the water bottling industry&#x27;s economic impacts considering its benefits and external costs.\nRegulatory Frameworks and Policy Analysis\nThe regulatory landscape in Italy and the European Union is oriented towards the sustainable management of water resources. However there is a need for greater consistency in enforcement and effectiveness. Current legislation and policies often prioritize corporate interests over public and environmental health. For instance while regulations such as the Water Framework Directive (Directive 2000/60/EC) aim to safeguard water resources and foster sustainable water management practices there are discernible inconsistencies in enforcement and regulatory gaps that have the potential to compromise these objectives thus enabling companies like Sanpellegrino to continue extracting water at unsustainable rates (Aukidy et al. 2012).\nTo address these challenges it is essential to introduce more stringent regulatory measures. This could involve the implementation of tighter water extraction limits and the enforcement of compliance through regular monitoring consequently contributing to the protection of water resources. Furthermore the involvement of local communities in decision-making processes concerning water resource management is crucial as it ensures that their needs and rights are duly considered (Bartolini et al. 2007). If meticulously employed these approaches may prove instrumental in amplifying the efficacy of existing regulations and driving meaningful change in the sustainable management of water resources within Italy and the European Union.\nRecommendations for Sustainable Practices\nA comprehensive strategy is essential to address the significant environmental impacts associated with the water bottling industry. Firstly as previously discussed stricter regulatory measures are necessary to mitigate these impacts. This entails imposing more rigorous limits on water extraction and ensuring compliance through continuous monitoring (Spiliotopoulou &amp; Roseland 2020). These regulations should be based on scientific evaluations of sustainable extraction rates.\nSecondly community engagement in decision-making processes related to water resource management is crucial. As proposed by Spiliotopoulou and Roseland (2020) and grounded in the principles of Ostrom (1998) community-based water management models have been shown to balance corporate interests and public needs effectively. A more equitable distribution of resources can be achieved by actively involving residents businesses and other stakeholders in water management decisions.\nThirdly companies should invest significantly in conservation technologies including developing methods for recycling and reusing water within bottling plants. This approach can reduce water usage and enhance the efficiency of water extraction processes (Spiliotopoulou &amp; Roseland 2020). The incorporation of these advanced technologies not only demonstrates a commitment to sustainable practices but also aligns with the growing societal and environmental expectations for corporate responsibility and stewardship. Therefore companies that invest in these conservation technologies are better positioned to minimize their environmental footprint and positively impact their operational costs and long-term sustainability.\nFurthermore corporate environmental responsibility initiatives must be prioritized within the business world. Companies like Sanpellegrino must lead in promoting environmental sustainability and social equity. For example they could consider allocating resources to funding local water conservation projects and supporting infrastructure improvements (Bae &amp; Kim 2022). This kind of proactive approach not only benefits the environment but also positively impacts the communities in which these companies operate.\nEmploying a multi-faceted approach to address environmental impacts by implementing rigorous regulations engaging with communities fostering technological innovation and promoting corporate responsibility initiatives is paramount (Spiliotopoulou &amp; Roseland 2020; McNabb 2019; Bae &amp; Kim 2022). This approach can substantially contribute to the sustainable management of water resources advocating for environmental sustainability and social equity. Additionally analyzing the water bottling industry&#x27;s environmental social and economic impacts highlights the broader implications of treating water as a commercial commodity. By reorienting the management of water resources to prioritize sustainability and equity we can ensure the long-term health of both the environment and local communities. Achieving this objective requires a collaborative effort among corporations governments and communities to develop and implement strategies that harmonize economic benefits with environmental and social responsibilities.\n\nReferences\nAukidy M. Deal: An Industrial \nPolicy for a Just Transition \nin Europe\n\nMario Pianta1    and Matteo Lucchese2\n\nAbstract\nThe European Green Deal (EGD), launched by the European Commission in December 2019, \nis a major policy package addressing climate change and aiming at a \u201cjust and inclusive\u201d transi-\ntion. Several shortcomings can be identified in the EGD: it lacks a vision of a just, post- carbon \neconomy for Europe; available resources are inadequate to reach stated objectives; and im-\nplementation tools are limited. We argue that making Europe\u2019s production systems carbon \nneutral would require a broader range of \u201cgreen\u201d industrial policies that need to jointly address \nenvironmental sustainability, structural change, and fairness of economic outcomes in Europe.\n\nJEL Classification: L5,  O2,  P48\n\nKeywords\nEurope,  climate change,  industrial policy,  structural change\n\n1. Introduction\nOn December 2019, the European Commission launched its proposal for a European Green Deal \n(EGD), a wide- ranging package of legislation with the aim to address climate change and to ensure a \n\u201cjust and inclusive\u201d transition in the European Union (EU). Unfortunately, the policies set out in this \npackage are not equipped to put Europe on a long- term socioecological path, with a radical change \nwith past trajectories and tools for fair and sustainable economic outcomes across countries.\n\nA growing literature has addressed the consequences of climate change and the challenges of \na socioecological transition.1 Several authors have tried to define huge industrial and financial \n\n1\u200aSee Aiginger and Schratzenstaller (2016) for a review of the main challenges to set Europe on a socio- ecological tran-\nsition path; on the challenges in facing energy transition, see Altvater and Mahnkopf (2018).\n\n1Scuola Normale Superiore, Florence, Toscana, Italy\n2Istat, Rome, Italy\n\nDate received: February 18, 2020\nDate accepted: May 20, 2020\n\nCorresponding Author: \nMario Pianta, Scuola Normale Superiore, Florence, 50123, Italy.\nEmail:  mario. pianta@ sns. it\n\nConference Proceeding\n\nReview of Radical Political Economics\n2020, Vol. 00(0) 1\u20139\n\n\u00a9 The Author(s) 2020\nArticle reuse guidelines:\n\n sagepub. com/ journals-  permissions\n DOI:  10. 1177/ 0486 6134 20938207\n\n journals. sagepub. com/ home/ rrp\n\nhttps://orcid.org/0000-0002-4111-0447\nmailto:mario.pianta@sns.it\nhttps://journals.sagepub.com/home/rrp\nhttp://crossmark.crossref.org/dialog/?doi=10.1177%2F0486613420938207&amp;domain=pdf&amp;date_stamp=
